Such a unique and “near-ideal” micro/nanocomposite architecture has been achieved via a facile/cost-effective route by facilitating heterogeneous nucleation/growth of Sb-oxide particles on/around dispersed rGO sheets upon incorporation of the same directly into the precursor suspension, followed by the reduction of Sb-oxide to Sb, in intimate contact with the rGO, during the subsequent single heat-treatment step. As a potential anode material for Na-ion batteries, the as-developed Sb/rGO composite exhibits a reversible Na-storage capacity of ∼550 mAh/g (@ 0.2 A/g) and a fairly high first cycle Coulombic efficiency (CE) of ∼79%, with the good reversibility being attributed to the coarse particle size of Sb and encompassing of rGO sheets inside the Sb particles. Furthermore, despite the coarse particle size, the Sb/rGO-based electrode exhibits outstanding cyclic stability, with negligible capacity fade up to 150 cycles (viz., ∼97% capacity retention), and rate capability, with &gt;86% capacity being obtained upon raising the current density from 0.1 to 2 A/g, resulting in a capacity of ∼490 mAh/g, even at 2 A/g.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D45296%26pageCount%3D12%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DAmardeep%2BAmardeep%252C%2BRashmi%2BC.%2BShende%252C%2BPranay%2BGandharapu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D14%26issueNum%3D40%26contentID%3Dacsami.2c11165%26title%3DFaceted%2BAntimony%2BParticles%2Bwith%2BInteriors%2BReinforced%2Bwith%2BReduced%2BGraphene%2BOxide%2Bas%2BHigh-Performance%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D12%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D45307%26publicationDate%3DOctober%2B2022" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2022 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291442&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Anode materials"> Anode materials </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites"> Composites </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties"> Electrical properties </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Micro%2Fnanocomposite+Architecture&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_micro%2Fnanocomposite%20architecture">micro/nanocomposite architecture</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Antimony%2Frgo&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_antimony%2FrGO">antimony/rGO</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Reinforcement+Efficacy&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_reinforcement%20efficacy">reinforcement efficacy</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode+Material&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode%20material">anode material</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Achieving good reinforcing efficacy in a composite necessitates proper distribution and dispersion of the reinforcement phase within the matrix material. For example, in carbon fiber or graphenic carbon reinforced particulate composites, the mere presence of carbonaceous reinforcement phase(s) on and around the particles (as is usually achieved) typically leads to marginal improvements in the properties, which fall behind the expectations in a big way. For such composite types, the ideal composite architecture would correspond to the presence of the carbonaceous reinforcement phase inside the functional particles; however, this has possibly never been achieved before and, thus, forms the focus of this work.</div><div class="NLM_p">Among various possible applications, particulate composites form an important class of next-generation electrode materials for alkali metal-ion batteries (such as, Li-ion, Na-ion, K-ion battery systems). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 1 to 13">(1−13)</a> This is particularly so for the “alloying reaction”-based anode materials, which suffer from stress-induced mechanical degradation due to the huge volume expansion/contraction during electrochemical alkali metal-ion insertion/removal. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14,15)</a> In the context of sustainable alkali metal-ion battery technology, the Na-ion battery system is believed to be the forerunner to replace or supplement the Li-ion battery systems (for various applications) due to the abundance of sodium resources in the Earth’s crust (unlike lithium resources) and its cost-effective nature. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 6 to 10">(6−10)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">However, one of the major issues associated with the Na-ion battery technology is that graphitic carbon does not host Na ions in its lattice and, hence, cannot be used as an anode material (unlike in the Li-ion battery systems). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> Accordingly, “taming” alloying-reaction-based anode materials for use as stable anode materials in Na-ion cells is of immense importance; this will also bestow the Na-ion battery system with higher energy density and improved safety aspects. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18,19)</a> In order to render the alloying-reaction-based anode materials structurally/mechanically/electrochemically stable over multiple electrochemical sodiation/desodiation cycles, the development of “proper” composites with carbon nanotube or graphenic carbon as reinforcement assumes a lot of significance.</div><div class="NLM_p">Among the alloying-reaction-based anode materials for Na-ion batteries, antimony (Sb) is a potential candidate, because of its high theoretical capacity (viz., 660 mAh/g) and a Na-storage potential that eliminates the risk of sodium plating during sodiation and the associated safety issues. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 18 to 21">(18−21)</a> However, it does suffer from pulverization/disintegration and detachment from current collector, because of the huge volume changes during sodiation/desodiation and the concomitant stress development, thereby often causing rapid fade in Na-storage capacity during electrochemical cycling of Sb-based electrodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 19, 22 and 23">(19,22,23)</a> As mentioned above, one of the ways to overcome this drawback is by forming composites with carbonaceous materials (especially with graphene-based materials), which may help improve the mechanical integrity; but provided the anchoring and distribution between the Sb particles and the carbonaceous materials are properly engineered. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 19, 24 and 25">(19,24,25)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">In this context, previous studies have demonstrated that Sb/C composites, prepared by ball-milling of Sb and carbon, can exhibit reversible capacities between 380 and 610 mAh/g; however, with poor capacity retention and rate performances, especially for those exhibiting higher first cycle capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 26 to 28">(26−28)</a> Zhuo et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> reported the development of a composite containing Sb nanoparticles and multiwalled carbon nanotubes via wet ball-milling, followed by drying; which exhibited an initial capacity of ∼502 mAh/g, with only 76% capacity retention after 120 cycles. Moreover, Wu et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> developed Sb with a rodlike morphology, composite of which with carbon exhibited a capacity ∼520 mAh/g with a ∼ 94% capacity retention after 200 cycles @ 0.1 A/g current density; but with the capacity decreasing to 404 mAh/g at 0.5 A/g. Shuai et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> developed Sb/graphene composite via electrochemical cathodic corrosion method, which exhibited a high initial charge capacity of ∼635 mAh/g, with a capacity retention of ∼80% after 150 cycles, albeit at a low current density of 0.066 A/g (i.e.,@ 0.1 C). However, the capacities dropped to ∼473 and ∼405 mAh/g at higher current densities of 1.32 and 3.3 A/g (i.e., @ 2 and 5 C), respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p">Even though researchers have developed a few different Sb/C-based composites via various synthesis routes that exhibit some or the other good performance as an anode material for Na-ion batteries, simultaneous exhibitions of high Na-storage capacity, outstanding cyclic stability and exceptional rate performance have not been achieved to date. Furthermore, the usage of Sb-based nanoparticles and carbon-based nanomaterials, together, is bound to reduce the tap density from a practical standpoint and also exacerbate irreversible capacity losses (and impedance buildup) due to deleterious reactions at the electrode/electrolyte interface. In addition, a careful look at the phase assemblage and composite architecture of the Sb/C-based composites developed to date indicate that the carbonaceous material remains around the Sb particles without reinforcing the particle interiors (hence, no impact on the mechanical integrity) and with considerable doubt over the anchoring between the Sb particles and carbonaceous material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 26 to 36">(26−36)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">In order to address the above issues, from a generic perspective, here we report the development of a reduced graphene oxide (rGO)-reinforced Sb composite, with the rGO flakes reinforcing the interiors of the Sb particles. This has been achieved via an innovative, but facile and cost-effective, route, which involves only a single heat-treatment step and invokes the fundamental principles of heterogeneous nucleation/growth of the matrix phase (here, Sb particles; via Sb-oxide intermediate) around the reinforcing (well-dispersed) rGO flakes, as well as Ostwald ripening. The as-developed Sb/rGO composite, characterized by the unique and “near-ideal” micro/nanocomposite architecture (of having rGO flakes inside the Sb particles), exhibited outstanding performances as a potential anode material for Na-ion batteries, in terms of all three performance metrics, viz., Na-storage capacity, rate capability, and cyclic stability; exhibiting nearly no capacity fade over long-term cycling and negligible drop in capacity, even upon increasing current density by more than an order of magnitude. Furthermore, because of the “true reinforcement” achieved with rGO, all of the above could be achieved without the need for fine-sized Sb particles; which, in the practical context, can be helpful toward the tap density and suppression of deleterious irreversible reactions at the electrode/electrolyte interface (as also seen here). From a more generic perspective, the route adopted here for developing the Sb/rGO composite, due to its simplicity and invoking of fundamental principles to achieve the “near-ideal” composite architecture, can be potentially extended toward the development of other composite materials, possessing similar architecture; and, thus, facilitating harnessing of the beneficial effects of “true reinforcement” for a variety of materials/applications.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  EXPERIMENTAL DETAILS</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Materials Preparation</h3><div id="sec2_1_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.1.1.  Synthesis and Basic Characteristics of the Reduced Graphene Oxide (rGO)</h4><div class="NLM_p last">The method for graphene oxide (GO) synthesis, as developed by Marcano et al., <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> was adopted in this work to obtain highly oxidized GO. To start with, 1 g of fine graphite powder and 6 g of KMnO<sub>4</sub> were mixed in an acidic medium containing H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> and H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> in a 9:1 ratio and stirred at 50 °C for 12 h, yielding a thick brown suspension. The as-obtained suspension was then poured into 120 mL of deionized (DI) water, followed by the addition of H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> drop by drop until color of the suspension changed to yellowish-orange. The suspension was then diluted with DI water and washed with HCl multiple times to remove the metal ions; it then was subjected to washing/centrifuging with DI water and ethanol until a pH of ≥5 was obtained. This was followed by drying in a vacuum oven for 12 h at 60 °C to obtain highly oxidized GO. The as-obtained GO was thermally reduced to a highly exfoliated reduced graphene oxide (rGO) by subjecting it to thermal treatment at 600 °C (using a heating rate of 10 °C/min) for 2 h in an inert (argon) atmosphere.</div></div><div id="sec2_1_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.1.2.  Synthesis of the Antimony–rGO (Sb/rGO) Composite with Unique Architecture</h4><div class="NLM_p last">To synthesize the rGO-reinforced Sb composite, with rGO being present inside the Sb particles, a facile and fast route, involving a single heat-treatment step was developed. To start with, 100 mg of the as-synthesized rGO (as above) was dispersed in 150 mL of ethanol by ultrasonication for 40 min. Simultaneously, a separate solution was prepared by dissolving 1 g of SbCl<sub>3</sub> in 30 mL of ethanol and stirring for 10 min at room temperature, resulting in a transparent/clear solution. The as-obtained clear solution was then slowly added to the rGO-ethanol suspension and kept for stirring for 10 h at room temperature. Subsequently, 5 mL of ammonia solution was added drop by drop to neutralize the acidic nature of the solution and cause precipitation; which was stirred for another 12 h at room temperature. The dispersion was then kept for settling and the as-obtained precipitate was dried in a hot air oven at 100 °C. The dried residue was subjected to thermal treatment at 700 °C (using a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup>) for 2 h in an inert (argon) atmosphere to obtain the desired composite, possessing unique morphology/architecture and phase assemblage. For comparison purposes, the same procedure was followed to synthesize Sb, sans rGO; viz., by dissolving SbCl<sub>3</sub> in ethanol, adding ammonia solution, causing precipitation, drying, and subsequent thermal treatment. The thermal treatment was also conducted at different temperatures below 700 °C, in the presence and absence of rGO, to understand the phase evolution and development of the unique/“near-ideal” composite architecture pertaining to rGO sheets being present inside the Sb particles.</div></div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.2.  Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Phase evolution of the materials was checked using X-ray diffraction (XRD) (Rigaku Smartlab X-ray Diffractometer), using Cu Kα radiation, over a 2Θ range of 10°–90°. In order to gain insight into the composition of the composite (i.e., the rGO content), thermogravimetric analysis (using STA 441 F1 Jupiter) was performed in air. The composite was heated from 50 to 800 °C to observe the changes in the mass loss. Morphology/architecture and phase assemblage of the composites and surface morphology of electrodes were observed using field-emission gun scanning electron microscopy (FEG SEM) (JEOL, Model JSM-7600F and Carl Zeiss, Model Ultra 55, respectively). The structural and morphological features of GO and rGO (as detailed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">section S1, and Figures S1–S3, in the Supporting Information (SI)</a>) were studied using XRD (PANalytical, X’ Pert Pro, with Cu Kα radiation), X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (Kratos Analytical, AXIS Supra), Raman spectroscopy (WItec, alpha300 RS; using a 532 nm excitation laser) and field-emission gun transmission electron microscopy (FEG-TEM) (JEOL, Model JEM 2100F).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">2.3.  Electrode Preparation and Electrochemical Cycling</h3><div class="NLM_p">The electrodes were prepared by taking the as-prepared Sb/rGO (as the active material), carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC; as binder), and carbon black (as conducting additive) in a mass ratio of 8:1:1, respectively. The mixture was ground in an agate mortar and pestle for 2 h, followed by the addition of DI water, thus forming the electrode slurry. The slurry was then coated onto the current Cu collector foil and dried in a vacuum oven at 90 °C for 12 h. The dried electrodes were then calendared, punched with a 12 mm diameter die, and used as the working electrode for electrochemical cycling in Na “half” cells. The active mass loading of the Sb/rGO composite in the punched electrodes was ∼2 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In order to evaluate the electrochemical performance of the as-prepared Sb/rGO electrodes as potential anode material for Na-ion battery, coin cells (CR2032) were fabricated by using the Sb/rGO composite coated on a ∼9 μm thick Cu foil as the working electrode, Whatman glass microfiber (GF/D) as the separator and sodium metal disc (of 15 mm width) as reference-cum-counter electrode. The separator was soaked in the electrolyte, which was NaClO<sub>4</sub> in 1:1 EC:PC (with 5 wt % FEC as additive). The coin cell assemblies were crimped at a pressure of 100 kg/cm<sup>2</sup>; and subjected to cyclic voltammetry (CV) scans, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy and galvanostatic discharge/charge cycles using Biologic (BCS-810) potentiostat/galvanostat and Neware battery tester. All the electrochemical testings were performed at room temperature.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">3.1.  Phase Evolution and Morphology/Architecture of the Sb/rGO Composite</h3><div class="NLM_p">XRD analysis indicates that the as-prepared rGO-reinforced Sb composite consists of the desired metallic Sb phase, sans antimony oxide or any other secondary phase (within the detection limit of the XRD; see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a). The peaks corresponding to rGO are not seen, due to the presence of high intensity peaks corresponding to crystalline Sb, which has a much higher atomic scattering factor, compared to carbon. Greater details concerning the basic characteristics of the as-prepared rGO, which has been found to be made of ∼10 constituent graphene layers, with an <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio of ∼0.83 and O:C ratio of ∼0.22, have been presented in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information (SI)</a>, in the form of a brief writeup (in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">section S1</a> in the SI) and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S1, S2, and S3</a> in the SI.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/medium/am2c11165_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/large/am2c11165_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern and (b) representative SEM image of the as-developed Sb/rGO composite (after heat-treatment at 700 °C), showing the unique and “near-ideal” composite architecture pertaining to the presence of reinforcing rGO flakes inside the Sb particles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/large/am2c11165_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c11165&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Thermogravimetric analysis conducted with the Sb/rGO composite indicated the nonoccurrence of any notable mass loss below ∼450 °C and above ∼600 °C; with the mass loss occurring only in the temperature range of 450–600 °C, where rGO gets oxidized and lost in the form of CO<sub>2</sub>/CO (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4 in the SI</a>). Note that <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a> shows a residual mass of ∼88 wt % at the end of the TGA scan, which conforms to antimony oxide (i.e., Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) and not metallic Sb. From this, based on the molar masses, the content of metallic Sb has been estimated, which turns out to be ∼70 wt %, with the remaining ∼30 wt % being rGO in the Sb/rGO composite (as further elaborated below Figure S4 in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a>). XPS data, as presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5 in the SI</a>, further confirms the presence of metallic Sb, as based on the typical characteristic peaks corresponding to Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub>, Sb 3d<sub>3/2</sub>, as well as a tiny amount of native antimony oxide, which is unavoidable due to surface oxidation during preparation/handling <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 38 and 39">(38,39)</a> and which, because of the much lower content, cannot be detected with XRD.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The most interesting outcome of the present research is the unique morphology/architecture of the as-developed Sb/rGO composite, as has been presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b. It can be seen that the rGO flakes/sheets are present, not only around the Sb particles, but also reinforcing the interiors of the well-faceted polygon-shaped Sb particles. To the best of the authors’ knowledge, such unique phase assemblage or “near-ideal” micro/nanocomposite architecture, pertaining to the presence of rGO sheets inside the functional (Sb) particles, has not been achieved previously, nor has such a facile wet-chemical synthesis-based route involving just one-step thermal treatment been achieved. From a more generic perspective, this “true reinforcement” of the particle interiors with rGO is expected to bestow the composite with the best possible reinforcing efficacy of graphenic carbon for various applications.</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11">3.2.  Understanding the Reinforcing of Sb Particle Interior with rGO</h3><div id="sec3_2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i12">3.2.1.  Phase Evolutions upon Drying and Being Subjected to Thermal Treatments</h4><div class="NLM_p">From a fundamental perspective, the development of such “near-ideal” composite architecture pertaining to rGO sheets reinforcing the interiors of faceted Sb particles indicates the involvement of heterogeneous nucleation/growth mechanism, where the rGO flakes/sheets act as the sites for preferential nucleation/growth of metallic Sb all around the flakes. The possibility of epitaxial growth of Sb on graphene sheets, albeit in a totally different context and with different forms of the materials, has been revealed in ref  <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p">To understand the phase evolution and the associated development of the unique composite architecture of Sb/rGO, thermal treatments have been performed at different temperatures below 700 °C, following the same synthesis procedure (as in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec2_1" aria-label="section 2.1">section 2.1</a> above) with/without rGO, and the evolution of phase and architecture have been looked into. To start with, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a shows the XRD patterns obtained with the samples prepared without the addition of rGO at different thermal treatment temperatures. It can be seen that the dried residue and that heated to 500 °C are made of cubic antimony oxide (Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>) phase, which got transformed to the orthorhombic phase of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> when heated to 600 °C (in accordance with literature reports <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 41 and 42">(41,42)</a>). If one looks closely at <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a, while the peaks corresponding to orthorhombic Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> are present at the right positions (i.e., at the expected diffraction angles, as per the standard), the intensity ratios among the peaks do not seem to agree with the standard. Such a mismatch in the peak intensity ratios is due to the existence of preferred orientation, as opposed to uniform/random orientation, in the concerned crystallites of o-Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>; which often gets exacerbated with faceted morphology, as in here. This depends on the crystal growth aspects, with the planes that form a major fraction of the exposed facets, due to preferred growth along certain crystallographic planes, yielding stronger intensity in the associated XRD pattern. Upon further increase in the heat-treatment temperature (up to 700 °C), in the absence of rGO, complete sublimation of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> was observed, with no residue left after the heat treatment. The occurrence of complete sublimation of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> between 600 and 700 °C is also expected based on literature reports. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 42 and 43">(42,43)</a> This immediately indicates that rGO, when added during the synthesis step, has a major role to play in the formation of Sb from Sb-oxide and retention of the same after heat-treatment at 700 °C.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/medium/am2c11165_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/large/am2c11165_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. XRD patterns obtained with (a) the as-synthesized materials, sans rGO, just after drying and heating at 500 and 600 °C; (b) the as-synthesized materials, with rGO incorporated, just after drying and heating at 500, 550, 600, 650, and 700 °C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/large/am2c11165_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c11165&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">In the presence of rGO, the dried residue and those annealed at 500 and 550 °C again showed the presence of cubic Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b). Upon annealing at the higher temperatures (viz., 600, 650, and 700 °C), the presence of rGO made a huge difference to the phase evolution by converting the Sb-oxide to metallic Sb, as can be seen from the corresponding XRD patterns presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b. This is in total contrast to the formation of orthorhombic Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> at 600 °C, followed by complete sublimation at the still higher temperatures, in the absence of rGO (as presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a and discussed above). The above differences indicate that the rGO, which got intimately mixed with the antimony oxide residue upon being incorporated right into the precursor slurry, acted as the reducing agent for the reduction of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> to metallic Sb. Hence, it is already apparent that the antimony oxide, and then the metallic Sb, evolved while being in contact with the rGO flakes.</div></div><div id="sec3_2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i13">3.2.2.  Evolutions of the Composite Architecture with Drying and Thermal Treatments</h4><div class="NLM_p">The SEM images obtained at the various stages of the processing (viz., dried residue and heat-treated at 500, 550, 600, 650, 700 °C), as presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>, provide a more direct visualization for the formation of faceted Sb particles from antimony oxide and encompassing of rGO inside the particles. To start with, SEM images of just the as-prepared rGO flake are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a, with <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>b and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c showing that the rGO flakes had been covered all around by relatively finer Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> particles (of ∼0.2–0.3 μm in size) in the case of the dried residue pertaining to the starting Sb-oxide/rGO composite. Upon being subjected to heat-treatment, the Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> particles tend to grow around the rGO flakes, apparently “consuming” the finer Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> particles, in compliance with the phenomenon of Ostwald ripening; as can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>d and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e, which correspond to those heat-treated at 500 and 550 °C. Here, the rGO flakes have a tendency to act as the heterogeneous nucleating agent for Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> particles to form and grow around them. For those heat-treated at these low temperatures of 500 and 550 °C, the typical octahedral-type morphology of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> particles <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41 ref44" aria-label="reference 41 and 44">(41,44)</a> (of ∼12–15 μm in size), reinforced with rGO flakes present inside them, can be seen (as in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>d and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e). It may be mentioned here that in one of our previously published works, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> a similar strategy was used to develop Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>/MWCNT composites in bulk polycrystalline form, with the MWCNTs reinforcing the grain interiors of the polycrystalline Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> matrix and, thus, leading to outstanding properties relevant to advanced structural applications.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/medium/am2c11165_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/large/am2c11165_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Representative SEM images of the (a) rGO flakes; the Sb-oxide/Sb-based composites, synthesized with rGO incorporated, (b,c) upon drying (i.e., dried residue) and upon being heat treated at the temperatures of (d) 500, (e) 550, (f) 600, (g) 650, and (h,i,j) 700 °C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/large/am2c11165_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c11165&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Upon increasing the heat-treatment temperature beyond 550 °C (i.e., at 600, 650, and 700 °C), the Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> got reduced to Sb (as per the XRD results) around the rGO flakes. This resulted in transformation of the typical morphology of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> to that of a multifaceted polygon morphology of metallic Sb particles (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>f–h). Of course, the morphology was not properly developed at 600 °C (as in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f), but became more distinct at the higher heat-treatment temperatures of 650 and 700 °C (as in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>g–j). More importantly, the conversion of antimony oxide to Sb clearly occurred around the encompassed rGO flakes, leading to the development of the unique composite architecture/morphology characterized by the presence of reinforcing rGO flakes inside the well-faceted Sb particles (∼30 μm in size) upon heat treatment at 700 °C (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>h–j); as also reconfirmed based on EDS mapping, as in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6 in the SI</a>. Schematic representation of the synthesis/processing route used, along with depiction of the underlying principles, leading to the unique and “near-ideal” micro/nanocomposite architecture, has been presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/medium/am2c11165_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/large/am2c11165_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Schematic representation of the synthesis/processing route used, along with the underlying principles, leading to the unique and “near-ideal” microcomposite/nanocomposite architecture pertaining to the rGO flakes reinforcing the interiors of the as-synthesized Sb particles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/large/am2c11165_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c11165&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i14">3.3.  Electrochemical Behavior/Performance as a Potential Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Battery</h3><div id="sec3_3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i15" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i15">3.3.1.  Electrochemical Behavior</h4><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>a and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b present the cyclic voltammograms (CVs) (@ 0.1 mV/s) and chronopotentiograms (at a current density of 0.2 A/g) obtained during electrochemical cycling of the Sb/rGO composite electrodes in Na “half” cells. Only one current peak can be observed during the first cathodic and anodic CV scans, followed by the appearance of three cathodic peaks and one broad anodic peak in the subsequent scans (as in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a). The potential profiles obtained during the charge–discharge cycles (as in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b) are also in agreement with the CVs, with a single potential plateau being observed during the first sodiation (or discharge) half cycle, followed by two close plateaus during the first desodiation (or charge) half cycle, with the behavior during desodiation (i.e., charge) remaining almost invariant in the subsequent cycles. The two close potential plateaus during desodiation, which indicate the occurrence of two separate phase transformations, could not be distinguished in the CVs, leading to the appearance of a broad current peak in the anodic scans. In contrast to the first sodiation (or discharge) half cycle, three potential plateaus can be observed in the subsequent discharge half cycles, which are analogous with the three cathodic current peaks observed in the CVs from the second scan onward. The overall electrochemical behavior appears to be dominated completely by the Sb phase in the Sb/rGO composite (as discussed below), with almost no apparent contribution from the rGO.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/medium/am2c11165_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/large/am2c11165_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Cyclic voltammograms for the first four scans at a scan rate of 0.1 mV/s, and (b) potential profiles for the first four galvanostatic discharge/charge cycles at a current density of 0.2 A/g, as obtained during electrochemical cycling of the as-developed Sb/rGO composite in Na “half” cells.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/large/am2c11165_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c11165&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The first sodiation half cycle seemingly leads to the conversion of crystalline Sb (cSb) directly to Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, viz., the final sodiated phase of Sb (possibly via some short-lived intermediate Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb phase, as per the literature <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46 ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 46 to 48">(46−48)</a>). The potential profile, current peak and the associated potentials (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) in the first sodiation half cycle are also likely to be partly influenced by the formation of SEI layer, although that seems to have been minimized here, because of the presence of fairly coarse electrode-active (Sb) particles (and, hence, low electrode/electrolyte interfacial area). The above aspects seem to be in agreement with other previous reports, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50 ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49 ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 49 to 51">(49−51)</a> where SEI formation occurs in the very first sodiation half cycle at a relatively higher potential, with respect to that corresponding to the initiation of sodiation of Sb, followed by the conversion of metallic Sb to Na<sub>3</sub>Sb at lower potentials. Based on the literature related to sodiation/desodiation pathway(s) of Sb, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46 ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 46 to 48">(46−48)</a> Sb/oxide composites <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50 ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49 ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 49 to 51">(49−51)</a> and the observations made here (with the CVs and chronopotentiograms), it is likely that during desodiation Na<sub>3</sub>Sb gets converted first to Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb and then to cSb, along with some amorphous phase(s), such as aSb or aNa<sub>1.0</sub>Sb. Accordingly, the second and subsequent sodiation half cycles also involved sodiation of some aSb, in addition to cSb. At first, the aSb (or aNa<sub>1.0</sub>Sb) gets converted to Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb, resulting in the first plateau/peak, followed by phase transformation of the Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb to Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb (corresponding to the second plateau/peak), followed by the conversion of the cSb component to Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb and then almost entirely to Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (viz, the final sodiated phase), resulting in the third plateau/peak. For better clarity, the sodiation/desodiation pathways may be represented by the following reactions:<disp-formula-group><figcaption><strong></strong><span class="figure__caption hlFld-FigureCaption"><p>1st sodiation:</p></span></figcaption><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/medium/am2c11165_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i16"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span></disp-formula-group><disp-formula-group><figcaption><strong></strong><span class="figure__caption hlFld-FigureCaption"><p>1st desodiation:</p></span></figcaption><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/medium/am2c11165_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i17"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span></disp-formula-group><disp-formula-group><figcaption><strong></strong><span class="figure__caption hlFld-FigureCaption"><p>2nd and subsequent sodiation:</p></span></figcaption><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/medium/am2c11165_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i18"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span></disp-formula-group></div><div class="NLM_p last">Nyquist plots, based on the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) data, obtained prior to cycling, after 1 full galvanostatic cycle and after 10 full galvanostatic cycles (@ 0.2 A/g) of the as-prepared Sb/rGO composite-based electrode have been presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7 in the SI</a>. The depressed semicircle at the higher-medium frequency region corresponds to a combination of resistance due to surface layer (viz, <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>) and charge transfer resistance (viz, <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), with the inclined line at the lower frequency regime being related to solid-state diffusion within the electrode material. Interestingly, the semicircle (at the higher-medium frequency) initially decreases in diameter upon electrochemical cycling. The initial higher impedance (i.e., prior to electrochemical cycling) is likely to be due to the presence of native surface oxides and relatively inferior wetting at the electrode/electrolyte interface, to start with, which get addressed upon electrochemical cycling and the passage of time. Nevertheless, the more important aspect is that after the reduction in the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> post-first cycle, only marginal increment is seen after 10 full cycles. This points toward an almost negligible buildup of the resistances associated with the electrode, which is likely to be due to the formation of stable SEI and retention of good electrical connectivity, as bestowed by superior structural/mechanical stability of the rGO-reinforced Sb-based composite electrode material under consideration here.</div></div><div id="sec3_3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i19" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i19">3.3.2.  Electrochemical Performance</h4><div class="NLM_p">With regard to the performance, the first desodiation or reversible Na-storage capacity, obtained at a current density of 0.2 A/g, is ∼550 mAh/g for the Sb/rGO composite (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>b and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a), which is higher than most reported to date for Sb/C composites at a similar current density <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56 ref57 ref58" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56 ref57 ref58" aria-label="reference 20, 26 to 32, 34 to 36 and 52 to 58">(20,26−32,34−36,52−58)</a> (see <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>), despite the presence of coarse Sb particles here. To understand the capacity contribution from just the Sb phase in the Sb/rGO composite, galvanostatic sodiation/desodiation of simply the rGO-based electrode yields a first cycle reversible capacity of ∼130 mAh/g (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S8 and S9 in the SI</a>). This indicates that the Sb phase, which forms ∼70 wt % of the Sb/rGO composite, contributes the bulk of the electrode capacity, which is ∼500 mAh/g (based on the Sb + rGO masses). This would, in turn, conform to a specific capacity of ∼700 mAh/g, as exhibited by the Sb phase, just based on the mass of Sb per se; considering possible errors in the estimation/measurement of the masses (especially in TGA), in a way, implies that the Sb phase in the Sb/rGO composite exhibits a Na-storage capacity (at 0.2 A/g), which is very close to its theoretical capacity.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/medium/am2c11165_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/large/am2c11165_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Variations of the specific Na-storage capacities and Coulombic efficiencies, with cycle number, during galvanostatic cycling of the as-developed Sb/rGO composite at a current density of 0.2 A/g in Na “half” cells (i.e., cyclic stability); (b) variations of reversible Na-storage capacities with current densities during continuous galvanostatic cycling runs (i.e., rate capability); (c) representative potential profiles obtained during galvanostatic cycling at the different current densities; (d) cyclic voltammograms obtained at the different potential scan rates (ν) of 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8, and 1 mV/s, with the inset showing the log(<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) vs log(ν) plots corresponding to the anodic and cathodic peaks.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/large/am2c11165_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c11165&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">solid-state reaction</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">527</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.1</td><td class="colsep0 (@0.1), 395 (@0.2), 263 (@0.5), 111 (@1)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center"> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference reduction</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">700</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.5</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">57</td><td (@0.2), 507&nbsp;(0.5), 459 (@1), 390 (@2)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center"> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center"> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">560</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.2</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">72</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">80</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">96</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">560 (@0.2), 385 (@1.5)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center"> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" rowspan="2" scope="row" align="left" valign="middle">Sb/Gr</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" rowspan="2" align="left" valign="middle">electrochemical cathodic corrosion</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">635</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.066</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" rowspan="2" align="char" valign="middle" char=".">77</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" 473.41 (@1.32), 405.09 (@3.3)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" rowspan="2" align="center" valign="middle"> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">475</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.32</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">500</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">72.9</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">spherical Sb/C</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">aerosol spray/pyrolysis</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">612</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.1</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">72</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">150</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">82</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">726 (@0.05), 632 (@0.1), 574 (@0.2), 498 (@0.4), 416 (@0.6) 340&nbsp;(1.2)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center"> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left"><b>Sb/rGO</b></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"><b>wet-chemical, followed by thermal reduction</b></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="."><b>550</b></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="."><b>0.2</b></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="."><b>79</b></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="."><b>150</b></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="."><b>97</b></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"><b>565 (@0.1), 556 (@0.2), 548 (@0.5), 542 (@0.7), 523 (@1), 490 (@2)</b></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center"><b>this&nbsp;work</b></td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="NLM_table-wrap-foot" id="IDtable-wrap-foot-tbl1-fn1"><div class="footnote" id="tbl1-fn1"><sup><sup>a</sup></sup><p class="last">Abbreviations used in this table: Sb, antimony; C, carbon; NP, nanoparticle; MWCNT, multi-walled carbon nanotube; PCF, polyacrylonitrile derived carbon nanofiber; 3D-C, three-dimensional carbon; Gr, graphene; and rGO, reduced graphene oxide.</p></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">Furthermore, a fairly high first cycle Coulombic efficiency (CE) of ∼79% was obtained (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a), which is among the best for alloying-reaction-based anode materials, generally, despite the presence of rGO (which is known to suffer from irreversible capacity loss; as also seen here in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S8 and S9 in the SI</a>). The good reversibility during the first cycle can be attributed to (i) the coarse Sb particle size, which minimizes the active surface area available for irreversible reactions at electrode/electrolyte interfaces, and (ii) a good fraction of rGO being present inside the Sb particles, rather than being exposed to the electrolyte. Of course, a Coulombic efficiency of &gt;98% was reached in the very second cycle itself for the Sb/rGO composite (unlike for the rGO, which took &gt;10 cycles to go above 90%, starting at a mere ∼25%; see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>), which has immense practical significance.</div><div class="NLM_p">Even though the coarse Sb particles are deemed to be beneficial toward the suppression of deleterious surface reactions (leading to high first cycle CE), such a dimensional scale is usually expected to lead to compromised cyclic stability (due to stress-induced degradations <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14,15)</a>) and rate capability. However, the presently developed Sb/rGO composite, having the reinforcing rGO flakes inside the Sb particles, leading to reinforcement in the “true” sense, is expected to exhibit very good cyclic stability and rate capability as a potential anode material for Na-ion batteries. In the context of cyclic stability, practically no capacity fade could be observed for the Sb/rGO-based electrode even up to 150 galvanostatic cycles @ 0.2 A/g; viz, exhibiting ∼98% and ∼97% capacity retentions after 100 cycles and 150 cycles, respectively (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a). Furthermore, SEM observations made with the Sb/rGO electrodes after being subjected to 150 galvanostatic cycles reveal fairly intact coating, sans delamination (as in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10 in the SI</a>). By contrast, ball-milled unreinforced Sb-based electrodes (i.e., sans rGO-reinforcement) exhibited comparatively very poor cyclic stability under exactly the same electrochemical conditions (viz, in control experiments; see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). This reinstates the highly beneficial effect of the presence of rGO flakes inside the Sb particles, as in the Sb/rGO composite electrode material under consideration.</div><div class="NLM_p">In the above contexts, it may be reiterated here that, while graphenic carbon is a good candidate for accommodating/buffering the dimensional changes and concomitant stress developments of “alloying reaction” based anode materials <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref24 ref25 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref24 ref25 ref33" aria-label="reference 19, 24, 25 and 33">(19,24,25,33)</a> (as for Sb), true efficacy of the graphene-based “buffer” is obtained only when there is a good anchoring of the same with the electrode-active particles. The presently developed Sb/rGO composite (with the unique phase assemblage) goes even one step forward in the sense that, not only is the anchoring good here, but that the graphenic carbon reinforces the interior of the particles, which is believed to yield a composite structure with elevated strength and improved connectivity within/across the particles. Furthermore, in the course of the evolution of the phase assemblage and morphology (i.e., during the material preparation), as per the SEM observations, the particles seem to have also developed some in-built porosity (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>, as well as <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a> in the SI), which is again known for accommodating/buffering stress developments of alloying-reaction-based anode materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref59 ref60 ref61" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref59 ref60 ref61" aria-label="reference 33 and 59 to 61">(33,59−61)</a> It is the above two aspects that are believed to contribute toward the significantly improved retention of mechanical integrity of the rGO-reinforced Sb electrode-active particles over multiple sodiation/desodiation cycles; and, thus, bestowing the same with outstanding cyclic stability, as observed here.</div><div class="NLM_p">With regard to rate capability, upon varying the current densities from 0.1 to 2 A/g (i.e., by more than an order of magnitude), an almost-negligible decrease in the reversible Na-storage capacity was observed, with a fairly high desodiation capacity of ∼490 mAh/g being achieved at the very high current density of 2 A/g, which is &gt;86% of the capacity obtained at 0.1 A/g (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c also indicates preservation of the potential profiles and, hence, the associated sodiation/desodiation mechanisms, even at the highest current densities used here. The improved connectivity within/across the Sb/rGO composite particles and good anchoring between Sb and rGO in the presence of the rGO flakes reinforcing the interiors of the Sb particles are believed to be the reasons also for the outstanding rate capability of the as-prepared Sb/rGO composite-based anode materials. Furthermore, even upon cycling at the high current densities (including 2 A/g) in the intermediate cycles, 100% capacity retention was observed upon returning to 0.1 A/g after the 30th cycle; which also points toward the stability of the anode material, even upon being subjected to fast sodiation/desodiation. <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a> presents the performances, as potential anode materials for Na-ion batteries, of various Sb/C-based composites reported to date, in comparison with the material under consideration here.</div><div class="NLM_p">In light of the outstanding rate capability of the as-developed Sb/rGO composite, to ascertain that it is primarily sodiation/desodiation in the bulk of Sb which contributes to the Na-storage capacity even at the high cycling rates, cyclic voltammetry was performed at different potential scan rates, viz, 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8, and 1 mV/s within the same potential window of 0.01–2 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d. Based on the CVs, the following relationship between the peak current (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) and scan rate (ν) was employed for the analysis:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/medium/am2c11165_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i21"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div></span>where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are adjustable parameters, with <i>b</i> being the slope of the straight line obtained by fitting the data points corresponding to a plot of log(<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) vs log(ν). The value of <i>b</i> indicates whether the overall electrochemical process is controlled by diffusion-based mechanism (for a value of <i>b</i> close to 0.5) or surface-mediated (pseudocapacitive) mechanism (for a value of <i>b</i> close to 1). In our case, the values of <i>b</i>, as estimated for the one anodic and all the three cathodic peaks by fitting the log(<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) vs log(ν) data points are found to be 0.51, 0.58, 0.67, and 0.72 (see the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d). This indicates that the overall electrochemical sodiation/desodiation process for the as-developed Sb/rGO composite, composed of coarse Sb particles with interiors reinforced with rGO flakes, is predominantly diffusion-controlled, in contrast to a previous report on Sb/rGO composite. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Furthermore, the apparent/overall diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i>; in cm<sup>2</sup>/s), as estimated here based on the CVs at the different scan rates, using the following relation:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq5"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/asset/images/medium/am2c11165_m005.gif" alt="" id="_i22"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(5)</span></div></div></span>where <i>i</i><sub>p</sub> is the peak current (in A), R is the universal gas constant (R = 8.31447 J/(K mol)), <i>T</i> the absolute temperature (in K), <i>n</i> is the number of electrons involved in a redox cycle, <i>c</i><sub>0</sub> is the molar concentration of redox-active species, <i>A</i> represents the surface area of the working electrode (in cm<sup>2</sup>), <i>F</i> is the Faraday constant (<i>F</i> = 96485.339 C/mol), and ν is the scan rate (in V/s), has been found to be, <i>D</i> ≈ 1.6 × 10<sup>–12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup>/s; which is higher than the value reported in ref  <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a>. The higher overall diffusion coefficient obtained here supports the outstanding rate capability exhibited by the Sb/rGO composite (as in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 6</a>b and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c), possessing the “near-ideal” architecture (as in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a>, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>, and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>), and also the inference concerning continued contribution from the bulk sodiation/desodiation mechanisms even at high current densities (as in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c).</div></div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i23">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">In summary, the development of Sb/rGO composite, characterized by the presence of well-faceted polygon-shaped coarse Sb particles (of ∼30 μm in size), with the interiors reinforced with rGO flakes, has been reported here. The unique and “near-ideal” composite architecture/morphology, pertaining to rGO sheets being present inside crystalline Sb particles, has been achieved by facilitating heterogeneous nucleation/growth of Sb-oxide particles on and around dispersed rGO sheets, followed by subsequent reduction of Sb-oxide to metallic Sb in the presence of the rGO, in intimate contact, during the subsequent heat-treatment step.</div><div class="NLM_p">As a potential anode material for Na-ion batteries, the as-developed Sb/rGO composite (having ∼70 wt % Sb and ∼30 wt % rGO) exhibited high reversible Na-storage capacity of ∼550 mAh/g (@ 0.2 A/g) and first cycle Coulombic efficiency of ∼79% (with CE becoming &gt;98% right from second cycle onward). Such a good reversibility is attributed to the coarse particle size of Sb and encompassing of rGO sheets inside the Sb particles. On a different note, despite the coarse particle size and predominantly diffusion-controlled reaction kinetics, the Sb/rGO-based electrode exhibited outstanding cyclic stability, with negligible capacity fade up to 150 cycles (viz, ∼97% capacity retention), and rate capability, with &gt;86% capacity being obtained even upon raising the current density from 0.1 to 2 A/g (viz, a reversible capacity of ∼490 mAh/g, even at 2 A/g).</div><div class="NLM_p last">Such outstanding performances are attributed to the unique and “near-ideal” microcomposite/nanocomposite architecture of the presently developed Sb/rGO composites, leading to “true reinforcement” with rGO flakes, because of the presence of the same inside the Sb particles. From a more generic perspective, the simplicity of the route adopted for developing the Sb/rGO composite and the invoking of fundamental principles of nucleation/growth of the matrix around the reinforcement are expected to facilitate the development of more composite materials, possessing similar architecture; and, thus, harnessing of the beneficial effects of “true reinforcement” (as observed here) for a variety of applications.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-2"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i24">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c11165?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c11165</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Characteristics of the as-developed GO and rGO (section S1); XRD and Raman spectra of the carbon-based materials (viz., graphite, GO, rGO) (Figure S1); XPS spectra of the GO and rGO (Figure S2); TEM images and SADP patterns obtained with the GO and rGO (Figure S3); TGA of the Sb/rGO composite (Figure S4); XPS spectra of Sb/rGO composite (Figure S5); SEM image and elemental mapping via EDS of the Sb/rGO composite (Figure S6); Nyquist plots (based on EIS data) of Sb/rGO composite electrode at different stages of cycling (Figure S7); cyclic voltammograms and potential profiles obtained with the rGO in Na “half” cells (Figure S8); cyclic stability and potential profiles of just rGO and unreinforced Sb-based electrodes in Na “half” cells (Figure S9); SEM images of Sb/rGO electrode prior to and after 150 electrochemical cycling (Figure S10) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c11165/suppl_file/am2c11165_si_001.pdf">am2c11165_si_001.pdf (1.01 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.2c11165" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Towards greener and more sustainable batteries for electrical energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Larcher, D.; Tarascon, J.-M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">19-29</span>CODEN:

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1755-4330</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Ever-growing energy needs and depleting fossil-fuel resources demand the pursuit of sustainable energy alternatives, including both renewable energy sources and sustainable storage technologies.  It is therefore essential to incorporate material abundance, eco-efficient synthetic processes and life-cycle anal. into the design of new electrochem. storage systems.  At present, a few existing technologies address these issues, but in each case, fundamental and technol. hurdles remain to be overcome.  Here the authors provide an overview of the current state of energy storage from a sustainability perspective.  The authors introduce the notion of sustainability through discussion of the energy and environmental costs of state-of-the-art Li-ion batteries, considering elemental abundance, toxicity, synthetic methods and scalability.  With the same themes in mind, the authors also highlight current and future electrochem. storage systems beyond Li-ion batteries.  The complexity and importance of recycling battery materials is also discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1039/C4CS00442F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Rational Material Design for Ultrafast Rechargeable Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rational material design for ultrafast rechargeable lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tang, Yuxin; Zhang, Yanyan; Li, Wenlong; Ma, Bing; Chen, Xiaodong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">44</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">17</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5926-5940</span>CODEN:
